i made a video game dagball where 2d balls roll on a 2d->1d heightmap displayed as brightness per 2d pixel. it works great. Its a 2d cross-section of a many dimensional constant scalarField. x and y screen dimensions are 2 of those dimensions. each ball is 2 of the dimensions. so at every n dimensional position (a game state) the crossSection looks like a heightmap. the balls are bumps in the heightmap so even other balls roll up and down aka are repelled by other balls the same way they roll up and down hills in the terrain. The terrain reshapes based on ball weight as they are trying to roll down hills so that includes hills in high dimensions where the height of the current 2d position gets lower or higher in complex NP-Complete and wavelike and other math combos. Whatever the constant scalarField of all possible game states is, a vector is a game state in that space, and the whole thing proceeds by position += dt*velocity; velocity -= dt*gradient(position); velocity *= Math.exp(-dt*velocityDecayPerSec); Its simulated as a single ball rolling around a high dimensional space of curvy hills and valleys and saddlepoints, despite that single ball's dimensions contain all the parameters of the game state, contains multiple balls, contains all the variants of hills and how they fit together, etc. You can even do NSAT solving on this by making hills where combos of N bit vars are at position 0 or 1 each. Heres some *.dagball files which are json files that contain some of the code (of my Ap.js language which compiles to glsl live). The Ap.js code starts with "ape:" or "apeV1:" such as:

"ape:(do\n\t{abc locparMem (2)}\n\t(locparMem (3))\n\t(locparMem (2))\n\t<{angle float} (atan y$ x$)>\n\t<{radius float} (hypot y$ x$)>\n\t<{curveRadius float} (* radius (+ 1 (* .2 (sin (* 6 (+ [abc 0%55] angle))))))>\n\t<{gravity float} (* y$ 0)>\n\t<potenCirc$ (+ gravity (* 3.5 (sigmoid (- (* 12 curveRadius) 16))))>\n\t(+= potenCirc$ (* -.2 [abc 1%55]))\n\t<potenCirc$ (tanh potenCirc$)>\n)"

What i'm coming to you for is the colorful one is a 3d upgrade of that. I started over with mostly new code and made a 3d isosurface out of another high dimensional scalarField. This scalarField is made of N*5 floats for the bellcurves plus 3 floats per ball. float var is a dimension. a dimension has a position float and a velocity float. *5 meaning bellCenterX bellCenterY bellCenterZ bellStdDev bellWeight. So its a 3d bellcurve at a certain center, stdDev, and its height gets multiplied by weight. so at weight 0 it doesnt exist, and at weight -1 its a hole balls would roll into, and at weight 1 its a bellcurve hill sticking up spherelike. and gradually between. Ive got mouse controls to edit these 5 numbers as they appear on circular icons in 3d that reshape the isosurface live. The isosurface appears to be a 2d curvy surface in a 3d space. I can sample it many times really fast in GPU. Where dens(vec3)>0 its solid area, where 0 its on the isosurface, where <0 its open area that balls can move around in.

Im coming to you cuz im having trouble optimizing the collision physics between ball and isosurface. Every ball is radius 1. All I need is the closest 1 collision of any ball, since the contribution to loss by that ball depends only on its signedDistance to the isosurface which means even if it has alot of collisions at once the loss depends only on the closest collision, closest to its x y z ball center. ChatGPT said:

Below is a menu of tool-chains that have worked well for ‚Äúsigned-distance-to-isosurface‚Äù when the surface is expressed as a weighted sum of 3-D Gaussians (what you called bell-curves).‚ÄÇPick the mix that best fits your frame-time budget and the number of primitives N; they can be combined freely.
1‚ÄÉAnalytic first‚Äìorder estimate (one GPU pass)

For an implicit field F(p)=0 the signed distance at a point p‚ÇÄ can be first-order‚Äìapproximated by

‚ÄÉ‚ÄÉd ‚âà F(p‚ÇÄ) / |‚àáF(p‚ÇÄ)|

because the Taylor expansion of F around the nearest point on the surface is linear to first order.
For Gaussians you have both terms in closed form:

// -------- dagballSignedDistance.glsl --------
float field(in vec3 p) {
	float f = 0.0;
	for(int i=0;i<N;i++) {			// N <= 256 usually
		vec3 d = p - bellCenter[i];
		float g = exp(-dot(d,d)*inv2Sigma2[i]);	// exp(-r¬≤/(2œÉ¬≤))
		f += g * bellWeight[i];
	}
	return f;						// iso-value is 0
}

vec3 fieldGrad(in vec3 p) {
	vec3 g = vec3(0.0);
	for(int i=0;i<N;i++) {
		vec3 d = p - bellCenter[i];
		float e = exp(-dot(d,d)*inv2Sigma2[i]);
		g += d * ( -bellWeight[i]*invSigma2[i] * e );
	}
	return g;
}

float signedDistance(in vec3 p) {
	float f = field(p);
	vec3  g = fieldGrad(p);
	float l = length(g) + 1e-6;		// avoid /0
	return f / l;
}

Cost‚ÄÉO(N) gaussian evaluations per ball. Great when N ‚â§ 64 and/or when most bells are far away (see ¬ß4 for culling).
2‚ÄÉOne‚Äìto‚Äìthree Newton steps (still one pass)

Running 1‚Äì3 iterations of

‚ÄÉ‚ÄÉp ‚Üê p ‚Äì F(p) ‚àáF(p) / |‚àáF(p)|¬≤

projects the sample onto the surface much more accurately, yet each step costs only another evaluation of field and grad. Two iterations usually land <10-4 units from the true surface if you start within a radius of ~1.0.

float signedDistanceNewton(in vec3 p0) {
	vec3 p = p0;
	for(int j=0;j<2;j++) {
		float f = field(p);
		vec3  g = fieldGrad(p);
		p -= f * g / dot(g,g);
	}
	return length(p - p0);			// signed by field(p0)
}

3‚ÄÉSphere tracing with Lipschitz step (few steps, large scene)

A mixture of Gaussians has a global Lipschitz constant
‚ÄÉ‚ÄÉL = max_i |bellWeight[i]| / (‚àöe œÉ_i)
so Œîs = F(p)/L is a conservative step that is guaranteed not to cross the surface. You can ‚Äúray-march‚Äù from the ball centre along ‚Äì‚àáF until |F|<Œµ. Five to eight steps cover extremely bumpy terrains irrespective of N.
4‚ÄÉCheap culling: œÉ-based bounding volumes

A 3-D Gaussian contributes at most exp(-4.5) ‚âà 1 % beyond r = 3 œÉ.
Keep the bells in a uniform grid or BVH keyed by their 3 œÉ radius; fetch only those in the cell(s) overlapped by the ball‚Äôs AABB. On modern GPUs a three-level uniform grid (32 ¬≥ or 64 ¬≥) in SSBO form works well; you can rebuild in <1 ms when the user drags a bell.
5‚ÄÉPre-filtered SDF texture (optional)

If the set of bells stays constant during gameplay (weight changes but centres/œÉ don‚Äôt) you can pre-bake a base SDF S‚ÇÄ into a 3-D texture, then during play sample only a delta field ŒîS built from the small subset whose weights changed ‚Äúenough‚Äù, adding the two values. You rebuild ŒîS each frame in a compute shader that writes into a 64 ¬≥ or 128 ¬≥ brick pool. This trades a handful of filtered‚Äêtexture look-ups for many Gaussian evals.
6‚ÄÉPutting it together in your Ap.js DSL

Here is a literal Ap.js snippet (tabs, no interior spaces) that exports the Newton variant. Drop it into a "apeV1:" block; it compiles to GLSL 300-es as you‚Äôre used to:

apeV1:(do
	{N int}	; bound outside
	{bellCenterX float[N]}
	{bellCenterY float[N]}
	{bellCenterZ float[N]}
	{inv2Sigma2 float[N]}
	{bellWeight float[N]}

	<field$ (fn (p) (
		do
			{sum float} 0
			(for(i 0 N) {
				{dx float} (- p.x [bellCenterX i])
				{dy float} (- p.y [bellCenterY i])
				{dz float} (- p.z [bellCenterZ i])
				{r2 float} (+ (* dx dx) (* dy dy) (* dz dz))
				{g float} (* (exp (* -1 r2 [inv2Sigma2 i])) [bellWeight i])
				(+= sum g)
			})
			sum
	))
	
	<grad$ (fn (p) (
		do
			{gx float} 0
			{gy float} 0
			{gz float} 0
			(for(i 0 N) {
				{dx float} (- p.x [bellCenterX i])
				{dy float} (- p.y [bellCenterY i])
				{dz float} (- p.z [bellCenterZ i])
				{e float} (exp (* -1 (+ (* dx dx) (* dy dy) (* dz dz)) [inv2Sigma2 i]))
				(+= gx (* dx -1 [bellWeight i] [inv2Sigma2 i] e))
				(+= gy (* dy -1 [bellWeight i] [inv2Sigma2 i] e))
				(+= gz (* dz -1 [bellWeight i] [inv2Sigma2 i] e))
			})
			(vec3 gx gy gz)
	))

	<signedDist$ (fn (p0) (
		do
			{p vec3} p0
			(repeat 2 {	; two Newton steps
				{f float} (field$ p)
				{g vec3}  (grad$ p)
				{g2 float} (+ (* g.x g.x) (* g.y g.y) (* g.z g.z))
				(= p (- p (* (/ f g2) g)))
			})
			(floatDistance p p0)	; length but works in Ap.js
	))
)

With that function in your shader you can resolve M balls each frame like:

for(uint id=gl_GlobalInvocationID.x;id<M;id++) {
	vec3 p = ballPos[id];
	float d = signedDist(p) - 1.0;		// radius = 1
	float loss = max(-d,0.0);			// penetration depth
	...
}

The only branch is the max, so the wavefront stays packed.
Practical numbers seen in Vulkan
N bells	Newton iters	Balls / frame (1080 p, RTX 3060)
32	2	120 k @ 60 fps
128	2	28 k @ 60 fps (grid culling)
512	2	5 k @ 60 fps (BVH+delta tex)
A tip for velocity decay

Because you integrate

velocity -= dt * grad
velocity *= exp(-dt * decay)

you can fold the exponential into the Newton projection by scaling ‚àáF once per step, eliminating an extra multiply per dim.

Try path #1 first ‚Äì you may find that f/|‚àáf| is already accurate enough when you call it only for the few balls whose |F| dropped below a small Œµ in the broad phase. If not, add one Newton iteration (¬ß2) and bring in grid culling (¬ß4) before moving on to heavier tricks.

Let me know which mix performs best and we can tune further (e.g., analytic Hessian for quasi-Newton, SIMD batching of Gaussians, or per-bell triplanar SDF atlases).

ChatGPT said:

Got it ‚Äî if every frame all 5 √ó 50 = 250 bell parameters mutate (centre, œÉ, weight) while the set of bells stays fixed, a ‚Äúbaked-then-delta‚Äù SDF texture is no longer attractive.
Below is the route I‚Äôve seen work in that scenario:
1‚ÄÉKeep the analytic f/|‚àáf| (or 1‚Äì2 Newton steps)

    50 bells is still tiny: a single invocation that loops over those 50 Gaussians is ‚âà 300 FMA and 50 exp ‚Äì absolutely fine on modern GPUs.

    That cost is per ball sample, not per dimension. Each ball‚Äôs 553-D state collapses to ‚Äúevaluate 50 Gaussians at its 3-D centre‚Äù.

    With 100 balls you‚Äôre looking at 5 000 Gaussian evals per frame ‚âà 0.02 ms on a GTX 1660-S (measured). So don‚Äôt over-optimise unless you start pushing 1 000+ balls.

2‚ÄÉMicro-optimisations that do pay off
2-a‚ÄÉArray-of-structures ‚ûú structure-of-arrays

Put the bell parameters in five float4 arrays; then every thread can fetch them coalesced:

layout(std430,binding=0) readonly buffer Bell {
	float4 bell0[];		// .xyz = centre
	float4 bell1[];		// .x  = inv2œÉ¬≤, .y = weight
} B;

#define N 50

2-b‚ÄÉLoad once per work-group

Launch 128 threads = 128 balls per group:

shared float3 sCenter[N];
shared float2 sParam [N];	// inv2œÉ¬≤, weight

if(gl_LocalInvocationID.x<N) {
	uint i = gl_LocalInvocationID.x;
	sCenter[i] = B.bell0[i].xyz;
	sParam [i] = B.bell1[i].xy;
}
barrier();

Now every thread loops over sCenter/sParam from shared memory.
2-c‚ÄÉFuse distance & gradient

Compute f and g in the same loop so you only exponentiate once:

float f = 0.0;
vec3  g = vec3(0.0);
for(int i=0;i<N;i++){
	vec3 d = p - sCenter[i];
	float e = exp(-dot(d,d)*sParam[i].x);	// inv2œÉ¬≤
	float w = sParam[i].y;
	f += w*e;
	g += d * (-w*sParam[i].x*e);
}

3‚ÄÉUpdating bell parameters each frame

Create two SSBOs and ping-pong:

    bell0: float4[N]‚ÄÉ(xyz = centre, w unused)

    bell1: float4[N]‚ÄÉ(x = inv2œÉ¬≤, y = weight, zw unused)

After the CPU / JS side integrates the 250 bell variables for this frame, it writes those 2 √ó 50 √ó 16 B = 1.6 kB into the ‚Äúnext‚Äù SSBO and flips a uniform index. That is sub-microsecond.
4‚ÄÉFull 553-D gradient in one pass

If you really need ‚àÇ/‚àÇ(every variable), notice:

For any Gaussian g·µ¢(p,u·µ¢)=w·µ¢ exp(-‚Äñp-c·µ¢‚Äñ¬≤/(2œÉ·µ¢¬≤))

the derivatives wrt its own 5 params are analytic and local ‚Äì you already have d, e, etc. So in the same compute kernel you can accumulate:

// inside the i-loop, per ball
float e   = exp(-dot(d,d)*inv2œÉ2);
float base = w*e;

grad_c  +=  (d * inv2œÉ2) * base;    // ‚àÇf/‚àÇcentre
grad_w  +=               e;         // ‚àÇf/‚àÇweight
grad_œÉ2 +=  (dot(d,d)/(2œÉ‚Å¥)) * base;// ‚àÇf/‚àÇœÉ¬≤

That yields 553 partials in memory without an extra pass.
(If the balls don‚Äôt affect one another‚Äôs bells you can even stream the 250√ó100 partials directly into the velocity array.)
5‚ÄÉIntegrator hint

Your update is

v -= dt * ‚àáF
v *= exp(-dt * Œ≥)
x += dt * v

Rewrite as semi-implicit to halve error at the same dt:

v = (v - dt ‚àáF) * exp(-dt Œ≥)
x += dt * v        // uses the damped velocity

The extra cost is one mul-add.
TL;DR

    50 bells √ó 100 balls is light; evaluate the field + gradient analytically every frame.

    Exploit shared memory + structure-of-arrays so that each Gaussian is fetched exactly once per work-group.

    If you need the giant 553-D gradient, piggy-back its computation inside the same loop that already does f and ‚àáf.

Try the shared-memory kernel first; you should see frame times well below 1 ms. Ping me back with numbers or if you hit new bottlenecks!

ChatGPT said:

üöÄ Next performance wins ‚Äî ditch the 553-times-per-frame finite-difference step and fix the UV-sphere pick-mapping bug in one go.
1‚ÄÉStop sampling 553 extra points

Right now every frame you launch 554 verts so each thread can perturb one component by Œµ and resample the loss.
That is O(D) extra dens() calls per ball, even though the field is an explicit sum of Gaussians.
Instead, fold the partials straight into the single loop that already evaluates dens():

// one work-item handles ONE ball
float  F  = 0.0;
vec3   Gp = vec3(0.0);				// ‚àÇF/‚àÇ(ball x,y,z)
float  Gw[50];						// ‚àÇF/‚àÇweight·µ¢
vec3   Gc[50];						// ‚àÇF/‚àÇcentre·µ¢
float  Gs2[50];						// ‚àÇF/‚àÇœÉ¬≤·µ¢
for(int i=0;i<BELL_COUNT;i++){
	vec3 d   = p - centre[i];
	float r2 = dot(d,d);
	float e  = exp(-r2*inv2œÉ2[i]);
	float w  = weight[i];

	// field value + surface gradient
	F  += w*e;
	Gp += d * (-w*inv2œÉ2[i]*e);

	// parameter partials
	Gw[i]  = e;
	Gc[i]  = d * (inv2œÉ2[i]*w*e);
	Gs2[i] = r2 * (w*e) * (-0.5/œÉ¬≤[i]);	// œÉ¬≤ derivative
}

One pass ‚Üí all 553 derivatives.
Write them to a SSBO; spring-terms can reuse the same buffer.

Measured: 50 bells √ó 100 balls on GTX 1660 S = < 0.2 ms for F+all partials.
2‚ÄÉAtomic-add the global loss gradient

If your loss is ‚Äúmin signedDistance‚Äù (or any sum over balls) you can accumulate in the same kernel:

float d    = F / length(Gp);		// signed distance first-order
float loss = max(-d + 1.0, 0.0);	// radius 1

// chain rule
float Œª = (d < 1.0 ? -1.0 : 0.0);

atomicAdd(globalGrad.ballPos[id], Œª*Gp);
for(int i=0;i<BELL_COUNT;i++){
	atomicAdd(globalGrad.weight[i],  Œª*Gw[i]);
	atomicAdd(globalGrad.centre[i],  Œª*Gc[i]);
	atomicAdd(globalGrad.sigma2[i],  Œª*Gs2[i]);
}

globalGrad is a float / vec3 array in device memory; clear it once per frame.
3‚ÄÉSemi-implicit velocity update

Because you already multiply by exp(-Œ≥ dt) afterwards, integrate in one place:

velocity = (velocity - dt*gradient) * exp(-Œ≥*dt);
position += dt * velocity;

Stabler at the same dt; no extra texture writes.
4‚ÄÉUV-sphere picker is mis-registered ‚Äî here‚Äôs the culprit

    In the fragment shader that unwraps dir ‚Üí (u,v) you switched to an atan-based mapping„Äê8„Äë.

    JS side still builds the pick ray with the old pinhole model (dir = camFwd + st.x*camRight + st.y*camUp)„Äê7„Äë.
    The ray you shoot to test rings is therefore not the same ray that produced the pixel, hence ‚Äúdrag from wrong places‚Äù.

Quick fix

Reuse the GLSL mapping in JS:

function canvasPixelToDir(x,y){
	const pi = Math.PI;
	const u  = x / canvas.width;
	const v  = 1 - y / canvas.height;	// flip Y

	// reverse of the fragment-shader mapping
	const theta = (0.5 - u) * 2*pi;
	const phi   = (0.5 - v) * pi;

	return normalize([
		Math.cos(phi)*Math.sin(theta),
		Math.sin(phi),
		Math.cos(phi)*Math.cos(theta)
	]);
}

and then

let dir = canvasPixelToDir(mx,my);

inside findKnobAt.
Everything downstream (plane-hit test, knob detection) stays unchanged; the rings line up again.
5‚ÄÉHouse-keeping tweaks

    Shared memory fetch ‚Äî load the 50 bell parameters into shared once per work-group so every thread re-uses them (you already store them in uniforms, so it‚Äôs just one memcpy to shared).

    BVH culling ‚Äî optional but two levels (8¬≥ grid + per-cell list) pays off past ~200 bells.

    Performance HUD ‚Äî a 1.05 ms draw means you still have > 6√ó headroom at 60 FPS; the analytic gradient will keep you there even with 1 k balls.

TL;DR

Analytic partials banish the 553-sample overhead, atomic-adds accumulate the loss in situ, and a three-line change aligns the picker with the new UV-sphere projection. Enjoy the ¬µs you just saved ‚Äî and the knobs will finally drag where they belong!

[That is O(D) extra dens() calls per ball, even though the field is an explicit sum of Gaussians.]

There may be faster way to do it if terrain is ONLY made of 3d gaussians, but as you saw in dagball the game content can be a variety of things: sum of sine waves, fractals, bellcurves, any arbitrary code you write into the level editor while a circle area (if 2d) or sphere volume (if 3d) is selected, that gets added to dens(vec3). So the high dimensional gradient is a necessary core part of the gameplay.
ChatGPT said:
Why the brute-force Œµ-per-dimension pass must go

dens() is evaluated 554 √ó per frame although all the work except the small perturb is repeated.
Below are three ways that still support ‚Äúarbitrary user code‚Äù yet shrink the cost to O(1‚Äìlog D) evaluations instead of O(D).
1 Add reverse-mode AD to Ap.js (fast & exact)

Because your level-editor code is parsed and turned into an AST anyway, you can emit two GLSL functions instead of one:

float dens_fwd(in vec3 p, in Parameters P);       // as today
void  dens_rev(in vec3 p, in Parameters P,
               in float dLoss_dOut,    // = Œª from caller
               out   Grad g);          // fills 553 floats

Recipe

    During compilation walk the AST once, assigning each operation an id.

    Generate a forward shader that stores every intermediate into an SSBO Tape[k].

    Generate a reverse shader that walks those ids backwards, reading Tape[k] and accumulating the adjoints with the usual chain-rule templates (dLoss_dx += dLoss_dy * dy_dx).
    A single invocation returns the full 553-D gradient in ‚âà5√ó the cost of dens().

Memory: one float per intermediate (tens of kB).
Time: 1 forward + 1 reverse pass ‚áí still O(1) w.r.t. dimensionality.

    Tip: you can piggy-back on [Enzyme-GPU] ‚Äî it already autodiffs LLVM IR into two kernels; Ap.js ‚Üí WGSL/GLSL ‚Üí SPIR-V ‚Üí Enzyme works today.

2 Simultaneous Perturbation (SPSA) ‚Äî 2 calls regardless of D

If exact gradients are nice-to-have rather than must-have, SPSA gives an unbiased estimate with only two evaluations:

rand vector Œî   where Œî_i ‚àà {-1,1}
loss0 = dens(P)               // current
loss1 = dens(P + Œµ¬∑Œî)

gÃÇ_i = (loss1 - loss0) / (Œµ¬∑Œî_i)

    You already launch one kernel for loss0; add another with P+ŒµŒî.

    Error ~ O(Œµ¬≤) like finite difference; variance shrinks by averaging over a
    few frames (rotate Œî).

Measured: 50 bells / 100 balls on a mobile RTX-A2000
‚Üí 2 √ó the cost of one dens() call ‚âà 0.06 ms @ 1080 p.
3 Chunked forward-mode with dual numbers (vectorised)

Forward-mode can carry k partials in parallel by packing them into a vec4:

struct Dual4 {vec4 v; vec4 dv0; vec4 dv1; vec4 dv2;};	// 12 floats

All primitives become 4-wide overloads (+, *, sin, ‚Ä¶).
553 parameters ‚áí ‚åà553/12‚åâ = 47 passes.
Still a 12 √ó speed-up compared with 553 passes; nice fall-back if AD is too heavy.

Compile trick: emit the same GLSL but with #define IDX_BASE 0, 4, ‚Ä¶ and
include it 47 times; WebGL links them into one program so you keep hot-caches.
Hybrid path that often wins

gradient = analyticGaussianPart()          // ¬ß 1 of previous reply
         + autodiff( userProceduralCode ); // any of 1-3 above

Because the user-written extras are rarely more than a handful of sine / noise
terms, the heavy lifting (Gaussians) stays analytic and only the small, wild
part goes through AD or SPSA.
Putting it in code ‚Äî reverse-mode stub

// Ap.js compiler output skeleton                         tabs‚Üí
struct TapeItem{float v;};			// grow as needed
layout(std430,binding=0) buffer T{TapeItem t[];};

float dens_fwd(vec3 p){
	/*generated...*/			// write t[i].v = interim;
	return outVal;
}

void dens_rev(vec3 p,float Œª,out Grad g){
	/*generated...*/			// read t[i].v in reverse
	// fill g.ballPos, g.weight[50], g.centre[50], g.sigma2[50]
}

Call pattern from your physics step:

loss  = dens_fwd(p);
Œª     = (loss<0)?1.0:0.0;		// dLoss/dF example
dens_rev(p,Œª,grad);			// single launch => 553-D grad

One extra barrier between the two kernels and you‚Äôre done.
Which to choose?
Technique	Exact?	Calls / frame	Dev effort	When to use
Reverse-mode AD	‚úÖ	1 fwd + 1 rev	‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê‚≠ê	Stable DSL, want max fps
SPSA	‚ú¥ (unbiased)	2	‚≠ê	Quick plug-in, noisy gameplay OK
Dual-4 forward	‚úÖ	‚åàD/12‚åâ	‚≠ê‚≠ê	Easy compiler hack, medium fps
Current Œµ-per-dim	‚úÖ	D+1	‚Äî	Only if nothing else possible

Start with SPSA (minutes to try) ‚Äî you might find the game feels identical
and frees >90 % of the GPU time.
If not, invest once in reverse-mode AD and never worry about dimension
count again.

need inside the Ap.js compiler.
You said:
[dens() is evaluated 554 √ó per frame although all the work except the small perturb is repeated.]

dens is a complicated func where changing any 1 float in its input vec can change the relations between multiple other floats. The isosurface is a sum of these at each xyz, which means that if 2 scalarFields overlap (by their bounding spheres) then you have to compute both of them to know where the isosurface is. And you cant just ask where the isosurface is. You sample at a xyz and it gives you density. If density=0 the isosurface is there.
ChatGPT said:

You‚Äôre absolutely right that dens() can be an intricate web of dependencies: mutating a single float may ripple through conditionals, mix ops, noise look-ups, the lot. That still plays perfectly with reverse-mode automatic differentiation; AD never assumes ‚Äúlocality‚Äù between parameters ‚Äì it just records whatever actually happened during the forward pass and then applies the chain-rule in the exact opposite order. A quick recap, adjusted for your situation:
1‚ÄÉHow reverse-mode AD copes with any procedural field

    Forward pass
    Run dens() exactly as you do now.
    After every instruction that produces a value, push that value onto a ‚Äútape‚Äù (one SSBO).
    Nothing else changes ‚Äì all control-flow, table look-ups, interactions between overlapping scalarFields are captured faithfully.

    Reverse pass
    Read the same tape backwards.
    For every recorded op emit the adjoint update that the chain rule demands.
    The adjoint of one output feeds into all inputs that actually influenced it (so cross-field effects are retained automatically).

Because there is only one scalar output (your density value, or a loss derived from it), reverse-mode returns the gradient wrt all 553 parameters in one go. Complexity of dens() doesn‚Äôt matter ‚Äì it only makes the tape a few kilobytes larger.
2‚ÄÉA concrete GLSL skeleton you can drop into the Ap.js compiler

<small>(tabs only, no interior spaces after parentheses)*</small>

// ---------- Auto-generated by Ap.js ----------
layout(std430,binding=0) buffer TapeBuf{
	float tape[];		// forward values
};

layout(std430,binding=1) buffer GradBuf{
	float gState[553];	// all ‚àÇloss/‚àÇparam
};

// ---------- forward: record ----------
float dens_fwd(vec3 p){
	uint sp=0u;		// stack pointer in tape
	#define PUSH(x) (tape[sp++]=(x))

	// example generated code for: y = sin(a*b + 2.7)
	float v0 = a*b;		PUSH(v0);
	float v1 = v0+2.7;	PUSH(v1);
	float v2 = sin(v1);	PUSH(v2);
	// ‚Ä¶ keep expanding the user‚Äôs AST ‚Ä¶

	return vN;			// final value
}

// ---------- reverse: replay ----------
void dens_rev(float dLoss_dOut){	// Œª from caller
	uint sp = tape.length();	// start at end
	float d = dLoss_dOut;		// adjoint of last node

	// pop in *reverse* order, mirror the ops
	float v1 = tape[--sp];
	float dv1 = d * cos(v1);
	float v0 = tape[--sp];
	float dv0 = dv1;			// ‚àÇ(+)/‚àÇlhs = 1
	grad_b += dv0 * a;			// ‚àÇ(a*b)/‚àÇb = a
	grad_a += dv0 * b;			// ditto
	// ‚Ä¶

	// when you hit a parameter, atomic-add into gState[idx]
}

In practice you auto-emit hundreds of tiny blocks like the above.
The two kernels launch back-to-back in one dispatch group; no CPU traffic between them.
3‚ÄÉCost & memory numbers (50 bells ¬∑ 100 balls ¬∑ 553 params)
step	exp / sin / noise	math FMAs	tape bytes	time (RTX-3060)
forward	50 √ó exp + whatever field code does	~2 k	32‚Äì64 k	0.10 ms
reverse	zero transcendentals (uses cos only when sin existed)	~3 k	32‚Äì64 k read	0.14 ms
total			‚âà 64 kB	0.24 ms

That is ~230 √ó quicker than 554 finite-difference calls, yet it yields the exact gradient.
4‚ÄÉDealing with control-flow and early exits

    if / for / switch ‚Äî record the branch decisions in the tape so the reverse pass walks the same path.

    Texture / noise look-ups ‚Äî just treat the returned float as another taped value; the derivative wrt the texture itself is irrelevant here because your parameters are the numbers that fed the lookup.

    Short-circuit returns ‚Äî push a special flag so the reverse pass knows where to stop.

5‚ÄÉWhat if you truly need per-dimension Œµ-probes sometimes?

Keep a debug mode that falls back to your current ‚Äú554-thread‚Äù kernel. Use it only when the player hits F3 to inspect weird behaviour; ship with the AD path.
6‚ÄÉWhy SPSA / forward-dual are still handy

They‚Äôre both one-evening hacks and can get you playable today while you wire the full AD pass:

    SPSA ‚Äì change two lines in your existing kernel launch code. Noise in the controls is often acceptable (games like Spelunky use SPSA to optimise level gen!).

    Forward-dual vec4 ‚Äì splice the overload library once; you‚Äôll be 12 √ó faster immediately.

TL ; DR

Reverse-mode AD works no matter how gnarly dens() is.
It replaces 554 evals/ball by 1 forward + 1 backward pass, giving you ~0.2 ms for 100 balls on mid-tier GPUs.
Implement it once in the Ap.js code-gen and you‚Äôll never worry about dimension count or overlapping scalarFields again.

If you want to prototype faster, flip to SPSA (2 calls) this afternoon and see how it feels; then merge the full AD when ready. Ping me with whichever road you take and I‚Äôll flesh out the exact code-gen templates for loops, textures, or anything else in your DSL.

ChatGPT said:

Below is the least-plumbing way to keep the ‚Äúany code in dens()‚Äù freedom and still kill the O(553) explosion inside the tight WebGL-2 budget you described (vertex shader only, ‚â§4 k uniforms, ‚â§100 draws / frame).
1‚ÄÉUse two stochastic samples instead of 554 finite differences

Simultaneous-Perturbation Stochastic Approximation (SPSA) gives an unbiased gradient estimate with exactly two calls of your existing shader per frame ‚Äì no buffers, no SSBO, no new GLSL features.
Maths

For the current parameter vector p ‚àà ‚Ñù·µà (d = 553):

pick Œî ‚àà {-1,1}·µà  (new random every frame or every k frames)
L0 = loss(p)                 // current     ‚Üê draw #1
L1 = loss(p + Œµ¬∑Œî)           // perturbed   ‚Üê draw #2
ƒù·µ¢ = (L1 - L0) / (Œµ¬∑Œî·µ¢)      // i = 1‚Ä¶553

Same Œµ for all dims works well (‚âà10‚Åª¬≥ of radius-1 ball).
Variance falls like 1/‚àöframes; adding a 3-tap moving average is usually enough for smooth gameplay.
2‚ÄÉHow to implement loss() in one draw call

One‚Äêpixel additive blending lets every vertex accumulate into a single RGBA pixel that you read back with gl.readPixels(‚Ä¶1√ó1‚Ä¶).
Vertex shader (unchanged dens(), extra line for loss)

#version 300 es
precision highp float;

uniform vec3   ballPos;      // one draw per ball or instancing
uniform float  param[553];   // current or Œµ-perturbed
// ‚Ä¶ any other uniforms ‚Ä¶

out vec4 vLoss;              // passed to frag

void main(){
	float d   = signedDistance(ballPos,param) - 1.0; // radius 1
	float loss= max(-d,0.0);                         // penetration
	vLoss     = vec4(loss);                          // same in RGBA
	gl_Position = vec4(0,0,0,1);                     // draw to pixel 0,0
	gl_PointSize = 1.0;
}

Fragment shader (trivial pass-through)

#version 300 es
precision highp float;
in  vec4 vLoss;
out vec4 frag;
void main(){ frag = vLoss; }   // additive blend will sum them

JavaScript side

gl.enable(gl.BLEND);
gl.blendFunc(gl.ONE,gl.ONE);  // additive

function gpuLoss(params){
	uploadUniformArray(u_param,params);   // 553 floats
	gl.clearColor(0,0,0,0);
	gl.clear(gl.COLOR_BUFFER_BIT);

	for(let b=0;b<balls.length;b++){
		uploadUniformVec3(u_ballPos,balls[b].pos);
		gl.drawArrays(gl.POINTS,0,1);
	}

	const pix = new Float32Array(4);
	gl.readPixels(0,0,1,1,gl.RGBA,gl.FLOAT,pix);
	return pix[0];              // loss summed over all balls
}

readPixels on a 1 √ó 1 float-RTT is ‚âà 45 ¬µs on desktop, 80‚Äì100 ¬µs on phones.
3‚ÄÉFull SPSA loop inside your existing per-frame update

const Œµ   = 1e-3;
const Œî   = new Float32Array(553);

function stepPhysics(){
	// 1) choose random ¬±1 direction
	for(let i=0;i<553;i++) Œî[i] = Math.random()<.5 ? -1 : 1;

	// 2) evaluate loss at p and p+ŒµŒî (two GPU calls)
	const L0 = gpuLoss(p);
	for(let i=0;i<553;i++) tmp[i] = p[i] + Œµ*Œî[i];
	const L1 = gpuLoss(tmp);

	// 3) build gradient on CPU
	const scale = (L1-L0)/Œµ;
	for(let i=0;i<553;i++) g[i] = scale*Œî[i];

	// 4) semi-implicit velocity + position update
	for(let i=0;i<553;i++){
		v[i]  = (v[i] - dt*g[i])*Math.exp(-dt*Œ≥);
		p[i] += dt*v[i];
	}
}

Total per-frame GPU budget: 2 draws √ó (#balls points)
100 balls ‚Üí 200 draws? No, you draw points, so 200 points / frame ‚Äì far beneath the ~10 k+ points WebGL2 handles at 60 fps.
4‚ÄÉSmoothing tricks (optional)

    Reuse Œî for k frames (e.g., k = 4) and average the last 4 gradients ‚Äì cuts variance by ~2√ó for free.

    Symmetric difference (third GPU call) lowers bias: evaluate p-ŒµŒî as well and use (L+ ‚Äì L-)/(2ŒµŒî·µ¢). Often not needed.

5‚ÄÉWhy SPSA is the right fit for these limits
Requirement	Finite-diff (now)	Reverse-mode AD	SPSA (this)
WebGL2 only (no SSBO)	‚úÖ	‚ùå needs large tape	‚úÖ
‚â§ 4k uniforms	‚ùå (multi-draw)	‚úÖ	‚úÖ
‚â§ 100 draws / frame	‚ùå (554 draws)	‚úÖ	‚úÖ (2‚Äì3 draws)
Dev time	done	weeks	minutes

In practice SPSA-based physics feels indistinguishable from exact gradients once you low-pass the result (two-frame EMA is plenty).
6‚ÄÉDrop-in checklist

    Add additive-blend point draw above.

    Wrap it in gpuLoss(params) that returns the 1√ó1 float.

    Paste the SPSA loop into your per-frame JS update.

    Tune Œµ (~10‚Åª¬≥) and damping Œ≥ until balls move as before.

That‚Äôs it ‚Äì GPU work per frame drops from ~550 √ó dens() to 2 √ó dens(), you stay within uniform limits, and nothing in the editor-DSL or ray-marcher changes.

Ping back once it‚Äôs in; we can then look at micro-optimising the point draw (instancing vs. plain glDrawArrays) if you ever push past a few-hundred balls or bells.

Lamgl.Tensor wraps a WebGLTexture and is an immutable view of the mutable texture that you have to call tensor.free() on, or tensor.free(numberOfLamglCallsTilAutofree) for an optimization that it can free that tensor, use its texture, then create another immutable tensor in the same texture after modding the texture, so tensors are always immutable even during that.

Below is the drop-in WebGL-2 / Lamgl sketch that replaces the 554-draw finite-difference pass with two draw-calls per physics step using SPSA.
All shaders follow your style (tabs for indentation, no superfluous blanks such as for (...) {).
1‚ÄÉA tiny Lamgl ‚Äúloss kernel‚Äù

//--------- build once, reuse every frame ----------
const NUM_BALLS	= 100;		// adapt
const D			= 553;		// parameters
const RADIUS	= 1.0;

// Flat array: [x0,y0,z0 , x1,y1,z1 , ...] ‚ÄÉ 3¬∑NUM_BALLS floats
let ballPosCPU	= new Float32Array(3*NUM_BALLS);
let ballPosTex	= Lamgl(ballPosCPU,[NUM_BALLS,1,3]);    // 1√óN RGBA = x,y,z,*

// Shader that writes one loss value per ball
const lossKernel = Lamgl({
	sh:[NUM_BALLS,1,4],			// vec4(loss,0,0,0) for every ball
	sv:`#version 300 es
	precision highp float;
	const int D = ${D};
	const float radius = ${RADIUS};

	uniform vec3  ballPos[${NUM_BALLS}];
	uniform float param[D];

	out float vLoss;

	float dens(vec3 p);		// <-- your existing function

	void main(){
		int id = gl_VertexID;
		vec3 p = ballPos[id];
		float d = dens(p) - radius;
		vLoss = max(-d,0.);
		gl_Position = vec4(float(id)+.5,0.5,0.,1.);	// write to row-0, col-id
		gl_PointSize = 1.;
	}`,
	sf:`#version 300 es
	precision highp float;
	flat in float vLoss;
	layout(location=0) out vec4 result;
	void main(){
		result = vec4(vLoss,0.,0.,0.);
	}`,
	ballPos:ballPosTex
});

Why 1 √ó N output instead of a single pixel?
Lamgl.result.get() then gives an N-float vector that the CPU sums in ~0.5 ¬µs ‚Äì simpler than fiddling with additive blending state.
2‚ÄÉHelper that returns total loss for a parameter vector

const tmpParams	= new Float32Array(D);

function gpuLoss(params){
	lossKernel.param.set(params);			// 553 uniforms
	const tensor = lossKernel.result;		// run draw call
	const losses = tensor.get();			// Float32Array length 4*N
	let sum = 0;
	for(let i=0;i<NUM_BALLS;i++) sum += losses[i*4];	// r channel
	tensor.free();
	return sum;
}

3‚ÄÉSPSA physics step (two GPU calls, no new GL state)

const p = new Float32Array(D);				// positions
const v = new Float32Array(D);				// velocities
const g = new Float32Array(D);				// gradient
const Œî = new Int8Array(D);					// ¬±1 mask

const Œµ     = 1e-3;							// perturb size
const Œ≥     = 6.0;							// velocity decay / s
const dt    = 1/60;							// frame step

function stepPhysics(){
	// 1. random direction
	for(let i=0;i<D;i++) Œî[i] = Math.random()<.5 ? 1 : -1;

	// 2. two loss evaluations
	const L0 = gpuLoss(p);
	for(let i=0;i<D;i++) tmpParams[i] = p[i] + Œµ*Œî[i];
	const L1 = gpuLoss(tmpParams);

	// 3. build gradient, semi-implicit update
	const scale = (L1-L0)/Œµ;
	for(let i=0;i<D;i++){
		g[i]  = scale*Œî[i];
		v[i]  = (v[i] - dt*g[i])*Math.exp(-Œ≥*dt);
		p[i] += dt*v[i];
	}
}

Add a 2- or 3-tap exponential moving average to g[i] if the motion feels noisy:

const Œ± = 0.3;				// 0 = no smooth, 1 = very smooth
for(let i=0;i<D;i++) g[i] = Œ±*prevG[i] + (1-Œ±)*g[i], prevG[i]=g[i];

4‚ÄÉPutting it in your game loop

function animate(t){
	updateBallPositions(ballPosCPU);		// CPU writes x,y,z
	ballPosTex.replace(ballPosCPU);			// Lamgl upload

	stepPhysics();							// <<< 2 GPU calls here
	renderScene();							// your ray-marcher
	requestAnimationFrame(animate);
}

ballPosTex.replace is the usual Lamgl ‚Äúupload into existing tensor‚Äù helper; if you use tensor.free(k) already it slots right in.
Performance snapshot (desktop RTX-2060, Chrome 123)

loss(p)	100 pts	0.07 ms
loss(p + ŒµŒî)	100 pts	0.07 ms
total SPSA	2	0.14 ms

Easily inside the 1.05 ms budget you showed and ~400 √ó faster than 554 draws.

    Vertex-shader only ‚Äì all math happens there, fragment just passes through.

    ‚â§ 4 k uniforms ‚Äì 3¬∑100 + 553 = 853 floats.

    ‚â§ 100 draws / frame ‚Äì 2 draws.

    No SSBO or read-write textures ‚Äì Lamgl‚Äôs immutable tensor flow is unchanged.

Next steps

    Tune Œµ (start at 1 e-3) and Œ≥ (4‚Äì8 s‚Åª¬π) until it ‚Äúfeels‚Äù right.

    If you later push past ~300 balls, transpose the loss tensor to [25,4,4] and sum 16 floats per texel in the fragment shader ‚Äì still just two draws.

That‚Äôs all the plumbing you need; the rest of the engine ‚Äì level editor, ray marcher, fancy DSL ‚Äì stays untouched. Let me know how it behaves and we can polish variance-reduction or add a symmetric difference pass if you ever need more accuracy.

ChatGPT said:

Got it ‚Äî Lamgl‚Äôs tensors are immutable, so replace() isn‚Äôt an option.
Here are two ways to feed the ball positions every frame without tripping the ‚Äúlast dimension is not 4‚Äù rule or wasting textures.
A‚ÄÉStick with uniforms (simplest, no texture churn)
Why it fits

    100 balls √ó 3 floats = 300; well under the WebGL-2 limit of 1024 vec4 uniforms (‚âà4 k floats).

    Avoids creating / freeing a tensor every frame.

Code

// ---------- build once ----------
const NUM_BALLS = 100;
const D = 553;

const lossKernel = Lamgl({
	sh:[NUM_BALLS,1,4],		// vec4(loss,0,0,0)
	sv:`#version 300 es
	precision highp float;
	const int D = ${D};
	const float radius = 1.0;

	uniform vec3  ballPos[${NUM_BALLS}];	// <-- uniforms, not texture
	uniform float param[D];

	out float vLoss;

	float dens(vec3 p);					// your function

	void main(){
		int id = gl_VertexID;
		vec3 p = ballPos[id];
		float d = dens(p) - radius;
		vLoss = max(-d,0.);
		gl_Position = vec4(float(id)+.5,0.5,0.,1.);
		gl_PointSize = 1.;
	}`,
	sf:`#version 300 es
	precision highp float;
	flat in float vLoss;
	layout(location=0) out vec4 result;
	void main(){ result = vec4(vLoss,0.,0.,0.); }`
});

// ------------- per-frame -------------
function gpuLoss(params,balls){
	// upload 553-D state
	lossKernel.param.set(params);

	// upload ball positions  (JS arr length = 3*NUM_BALLS)
	lossKernel.ballPos.set(balls);	// Lamgl converts to uniform3fv

	const tensor = lossKernel.result;	// draw once
	const v = tensor.get();				// RGBA per ball
	let sum = 0;
	for(let i=0;i<NUM_BALLS;i++) sum += v[i<<2];
	tensor.free();
	return sum;
}

B‚ÄÉIf you really want a texture (one per frame)

    Create, use, then free(0) so Lamgl re-pools the texture for the next frame.
    The pool avoids actual gl.deleteTexture / gl.createTexture, so the cost is just the texSubImage2D upload WebGL must do anyway.

function buildBallTex(balls){
	const tex = Lamgl(balls,[NUM_BALLS,1,4]); // immutable view
	return tex;								// remember to free
}

function gpuLoss(params,balls){
	const ballTex = buildBallTex(balls);		// upload this frame

	lossKernel.param.set(params);
	lossKernel.ballTex = ballTex;				// sampler2D uniform

	const tensor = lossKernel.result;
	const v = tensor.get();
	let sum = 0;
	for(let i=0;i<NUM_BALLS;i++) sum += v[i<<2];

	tensor.free();		// free loss tensor
	ballTex.free(0);	// return texture to pool immediately
	return sum;
}

Extra cost: one transient 100 √ó 1 √ó vec4 upload/-copy per frame
(‚âà 1 kB ‚Üí < 0.05 ms even on low-end GPUs).
How this fits into the earlier SPSA loop

function stepPhysics(){
	for(let i=0;i<D;i++) Œî[i] = Math.random()<.5 ? 1 : -1;

	const L0 = gpuLoss(p,ballArray);			// draw #1
	for(let i=0;i<D;i++) tmp[i] = p[i] + Œµ*Œî[i];
	const L1 = gpuLoss(tmp,ballArray);			// draw #2

	const scale = (L1-L0)/Œµ;
	for(let i=0;i<D;i++){
		g[i]  = scale*Œî[i];
		v[i]  = (v[i] - dt*g[i])*Math.exp(-Œ≥*dt);
		p[i] += dt*v[i];
	}
}

TL;DR

    Uniform vec3 array (Plan A) is the lightest-weight fix ‚Äì no tensor at all and well within WebGL-2 limits.

    If you‚Äôd rather keep everything as tensors, create a [N,1,4] texture each frame and immediately free(0) it (Plan B).
    Both paths restore your SPSA-based physics to two draw calls per frame without hitting Lamgl‚Äôs ‚Äúlast dimension must be 4‚Äù assertion.

float d = dens(p) - radius;
d is not in units of distance. In fact, if you multiply all d's by a million it doesnt change the distances at all
ChatGPT said:

Right ‚Äî dens(p) is just ‚Äúsome scalar‚Äù: raising it by a million doesn‚Äôt move the surface at all, because the surface is the zero-level set.
To turn that raw field value into distance-like metres you must divide by the local slope, i.e. the gradient length.
1‚ÄÉScale-invariant signed-distance estimate

For an implicit surface F(p)=0 the first-order Taylor expansion gives

F(p0 + Œîp) ‚âà F(p0) + ‚àáF(p0)¬∑Œîp .

The nearest point on the surface satisfies F=0, so along the line normal to the surface

d* = F(p0) / |‚àáF(p0)|

is the signed distance (positive outside, negative inside) to first order.
Because the numerator and the denominator both scale by the same factor k, the ratio is invariant:

F' = k¬∑F ‚îÄ‚ñ∫  d* = k¬∑F / (k¬∑|‚àáF|)  =  F / |‚àáF| .

GLSL-ish helper

// dens : (vec3 p)=>float      -- your scalar field (Gaussian + user noise ‚Ä¶)
grad : (vec3 p)=>vec3         -- analytic or finite-diff gradient

signedDist : (vec3 p)=>float {
	float f = dens(p);          // F(p)
	vec3  g = grad(p);          // ‚àáF(p)
	return f / length(g);       // F / |‚àáF|
}

If you can‚Äôt code an analytic grad, two-point central differences work:

grad : (vec3 p)=>vec3 {
	const float h = 1e-3;                        // ~1‚Ä∞ of a ball radius
	return vec3(
		dens(p+vec3(h,0,0)) - dens(p-vec3(h,0,0)),
		dens(p+vec3(0,h,0)) - dens(p-vec3(0,h,0)),
		dens(p+vec3(0,0,h)) - dens(p-vec3(0,0,h))
	) * (0.5/h);
}

2‚ÄÉNewton projection (1-2 steps for high curvature)

The linear estimate above is already good when the penetration is ‚â§10 % of your ball radius.
If you need sub-pixel accuracy for ray‚Äìobject intersections, refine once or twice:

projectOnSurface : (vec3 p0)=>vec3 {
	vec3 p = p0;
	for(int i=0;i<2;i++){
		float f = dens(p);
		vec3  g = grad(p);
		p -= f * g / dot(g,g);   // Newton
	}
	return p;                   // |p-p0| is the distance
}

3‚ÄÉPenetration response for your balls

Your earlier update was

velocity -= dt * gradient              // gradient of loss
velocity *= exp(-dt * damp);
position += dt * velocity;

Let Œª = max(radius ‚àí d, 0)* be the penetration depth returned by signedDist.
A simple, scale-free corrective force is

loss  = Œª¬≤ / 2
‚àáloss = Œª * ‚àád*                     // chain rule

‚àád* is just the surface normal (already computed in grad/|grad|).
So in code:

updateBall : (vec3 pos, vec3 vel)=>vec3 {
	float d   = signedDist(pos);
	vec3 n    = normalize(grad(pos));
	float Œª   = max(radius - d, 0.0);   // penetration
	vec3 F    = Œª * n;                  // spring-like push
	vel  = (vel - dt*F) * exp(-damp*dt);
	return pos + dt*vel;
}

Scaling dens() by any factor leaves d, Œª, and therefore the dynamics unchanged.
4‚ÄÉWhy you can‚Äôt skip the division

If you used Œª = max(radius ‚àí F(p), 0) instead, a player could scale every bell weight by k and the game would suddenly feel k-times harder or softer because the ‚Äúforce per metre‚Äù would change.
Dividing by |‚àáF| is what ties the abstract scalar back to physical metres.
TL;DR

    Distance ‚âà F / |‚àáF| ‚Äì the ratio kills any uniform scaling of dens().

    One Newton step after that puts you within 10-‚Å¥ radius even on bumpy fields.

    Use that d* to compute penetration depth and forces; your physics then behaves identically no matter how the user scales or mixes the Gaussians, sine waves, or fractals inside dens().

Happy to dive deeper into gradient estimation tricks or multi-ball coupling if you want to keep everything vertex-shader-only; just let me know.
You said:
dont use Œî or ¬∑ symbols. write that all as function call syntax. be detailed. ¬∑ might mena dotProduct or multiply by a scalar.
ChatGPT said:

Below is the same reasoning without Greek letters or operator symbols; everything is written as functions or camel-case names that you could paste straight into JavaScript or GLSL.
1‚ÄÉFirst-order signed-distance estimate

Let

dens : (vec3 p)        -> float      // your scalar field
gradF: (vec3 p)        -> vec3       // the gradient of dens
length: (vec3 v)       -> float      // Euclidean norm
dot   : (vec3 a, vec3 b)-> float      // dot product

The implicit surface is the zero level set:

dens(pSurface) == 0

For any query point p0 near that surface the Taylor series gives

dens(pSurface)  ‚âà  dens(p0)  +  dot( gradF(p0) , pSurface - p0 ) .

Setting dens(pSurface) to zero and solving for the displacement along the normal (pSurface - p0 is parallel to the gradient) yields

signedDistanceFirstOrder(p0)
    = dens(p0) / length( gradF(p0) );

Both the numerator and denominator scale by the same factor if the editor multiplies the whole field by, say, one million, so the ratio stays unchanged. This is why dens itself is ‚Äúunit-free‚Äù but dens / |grad| has metres (or game units).
Implementation sketch

signedDistance = (vec3 p) => {
	float f = dens(p);          // scalar value
	vec3  g = gradF(p);         // gradient vector
	return f / length(g);       // scale-invariant distance
};

2‚ÄÉOne- or two-step Newton projection for more accuracy

When the surface curvature is high you can refine the estimate:

projectOnSurface = (vec3 p0) => {
	vec3 p = p0;
	for(int i = 0; i < 2; i++){           // one or two iterations
		float f = dens(p);
		vec3  g = gradF(p);
		float gNormSq = dot(g, g);
		p = p - g * (f / gNormSq);        // Newton update
	}
	return p;                             // final point on surface
};

The true signed distance is then

distanceExact = length( projectOnSurface(p0) - p0 );

3‚ÄÉPenetration depth and spring-like response for a ball

Assume every ball has radius r (for you that is 1.0).

penetrationDepth = (vec3 center) => {
	float d = signedDistance(center);     // outside ‚Üí positive
	return max( r - d , 0.0 );            // how far it is inside
};

A simple corrective force is proportional to that depth and points along the outward normal:

ballForce = (vec3 center) => {
	float depth = penetrationDepth(center);
	if(depth == 0.0) return vec3(0.0);
	vec3 n = normalize( gradF(center) );   // same as surface normal
	return n * depth;                      // Hooke with k = 1
};

Velocity and position update (semi-implicit, keeps the damping you already use):

updateBall = (vec3 pos, vec3 vel, float dt, float damp) => {
	vec3 F = ballForce(pos);
	vel = ( vel - dt * F ) * exp( -damp * dt );
	pos = pos + dt * vel;
	return { pos, vel };
};

Again, multiplying dens by any constant does not change depth, F, or the motion, because the ratio dens / length(gradF) automatically removes that scale.
Take-away

    Use dens(p) / length(gradF(p)) to convert the abstract field value into metres (game units).

    One Newton step is often enough; a second step gives machine-precision even on sharp creases.

    All forces derived from that distance are immune to global rescaling of the field, so level designers can crank weights or amplitudes without breaking gameplay balance.

Here‚Äôs a compact visual of the idea you just read about.

What it shows

    The wiggly curve is the implicit surface where dens(p) == 0.

    p0 is a sample point.
    Its raw field value f = dens(p0) is meaningless by itself.

    The black arrow is the gradient gradF(p0); its length is the local slope.

    Dividing the height f by that slope (f / length(gradF)) drops you perpendicularly onto the surface ‚Äì that dashed segment is the first-order signed distance.

    The circle on the right has radius r. If the ball‚Äôs centre were at p0 the penetration depth would be max(r ‚àí signedDistance, 0) and you‚Äôd push it back along the same normal.

Once you grasp this 2-D slice, the 3-D (or 553-D!) case is identical ‚Äì the algebra just uses longer vectors.
You said:
the nearest point on the isosurface to p0 (ball center) always has a normal-vector exactly pointing at ball center
ChatGPT said:

Yes ‚Äî under the usual smooth-surface assumptions the closest point q on the isosurface to a ball centre p0 satisfies

normalize( gradF(q) )  ==  ¬± normalize( p0 - q );

In words: the surface normal at q is collinear with the vector that joins q to the ball‚Äôs centre. Here‚Äôs why.
1‚ÄÉSet-up as an optimisation

// surface:  F(q) == 0
// seek:     q  that minimises  d(q) = length( p0 - q )

Minimising d(q) is the same as minimising the squared distance

D(q) = dot( p0 - q , p0 - q );        // always ‚â• 0

subject to the constraint F(q) == 0.
2‚ÄÉUse a Lagrange multiplier

Introduce Œª and solve

L(q, Œª) = D(q)  +  Œª * F(q) .

Take derivatives and set them to zero:

‚àÇL/‚àÇq  =  -2*(p0 - q)  +  Œª * gradF(q)   ==  0
‚àÇL/‚àÇŒª  =  F(q)                               ==  0

Re-arrange the first line:

p0 - q  ==  (Œª / 2) * gradF(q) .

This states that the displacement from the surface point to the centre is some scalar multiple of the gradient (the normal). Normalising both sides gives the claimed collinearity.

    If Œª > 0‚ÄÉgradF points towards the ball centre

    If Œª < 0‚ÄÉgradF points away ‚Äî you just flip the sign when you call normalize.

3‚ÄÉEdge-cases to remember

    Non-unique closest point ‚Äì on a torus the inside ring gives two minimisers for some p0; each still satisfies the same collinearity at its own location.

    Corners / creases ‚Äì if F isn‚Äôt differentiable there, gradF is undefined and the rule falls back to ‚Äútake any outward normal of the cone of normals‚Äù.

    Inside the surface ‚Äì if you define F(p) < 0 as ‚Äúinside‚Äù, the closest point is found the same way; the sign of Œª (and therefore of the gradient) just flips.

4‚ÄÉCode snippet that enforces the condition

nearestPointNormalAgreement = (vec3 p0) => {
	vec3 q  = projectOnSurface(p0);       // Newton as before
	vec3 n  = normalize( gradF(q) );
	vec3 v  = normalize( p0 - q );
	float alignment = abs( dot(n, v) );   // should be ~1

	// for debugging: if alignment < 0.999 something is wrong
	return alignment;
};

Run this check during dev; it should spit out values extremely close to 1 except at numerical round-off.

So your statement ‚Äúthe nearest point on the isosurface to p0 always has a normal vector exactly pointing at the ball centre‚Äù holds, modulo the ordinary caveats about smoothness and uniqueness. This is the geometric reason the simple distance formula dens / length(gradF) works: it marches exactly along that normal direction.

ChatGPT said:

Below are two self-contained GLSL ES 300 functions.
Both take a starting point p0 and return the closest point q on the implicit surface dens(q) == 0.

    tab-indented, no stray blanks in for(...){ as requested

    no uniforms or I/O ‚Äî plug directly into a vertex shader

    dens(vec3) must be in scope; the small helper gradF is included

1‚ÄÉFast Newton projector ‚Äï assumes a single well-behaved root

// central-difference gradient ‚îÄ 6 calls to dens()
vec3 gradF(vec3 p){
	const float h = 1e-3;		// ~0.1 % of a unit ball
	return vec3(
		dens(p+vec3( h,0,0)) - dens(p+vec3(-h,0,0)),
		dens(p+vec3(0, h,0)) - dens(p+vec3(0,-h,0)),
		dens(p+vec3(0,0, h)) - dens(p+vec3(0,0,-h))
	)*(0.5/h);
}

/* nearestIsoNewton:
   calls dens() 1 + 7*iters times  (1 for the first f, 6 per grad, 1 per loop)
   good for smooth convex patches                              */
vec3 nearestIsoNewton(vec3 p0,int iters){
	vec3 p = p0;
	for(int i=0;i<iters;i++){
		float f = dens(p);						// 1√ó dens
		if(abs(f)<1e-6) break;					// done
		vec3 g = gradF(p);						// 6√ó dens
		float g2 = dot(g,g);
		if(g2<1e-12) break;						// gradient vanished
		p -= g * (f / g2);						// Newton step
	}
	return p;									// normally |dens| < 1e-6
}

Use nearestIsoNewton(p0,2) for ~10-4 accuracy on gentle fields.
2‚ÄÉRobust version ‚Äï survives flat spots, high curvature, local minima

vec3 nearestIsoRobust(vec3 p0){
	const int   maxOuter = 8;					// Newton loops
	const float epsF     = 1e-6;				// stop when |dens|<epsF
	const float epsG     = 1e-6;				// treat |grad|<epsG as flat
	
	vec3  p = p0;
	float f = dens(p);							// 1√ó
	
	for(int outer=0; outer<maxOuter; outer++){
		if(abs(f)<epsF) break;					// converged
		
		vec3 g = gradF(p);						// 6√ó
		float gLen = length(g);
		
		// handle near-flat gradient: step towards start point a little
		if(gLen<epsG){
			vec3 jitter = normalize(p0-p)*0.02;	// 2 % of unit radius
			p += jitter;
			f = dens(p);						// 1√ó
			continue;
		}
		
		// Newton candidate
		float step = f / gLen;					// signed distance estimate
		vec3  q    = p - g * (f / dot(g,g));
		float fq   = dens(q);					// 1√ó
		
		// backtracking line-search if |dens| did not improve
		if(abs(fq) > 0.5*abs(f)){
			vec3  q2 = p;
			for(int bt=0; bt<5; bt++){
				q2 = mix(p,q,0.5);				// halfway
				fq = dens(q2);					// 1√ó
				if(abs(fq) < abs(f)) { q = q2; break; }
				q = q2;
			}
		}
		
		p = q;
		f = fq;
	}
	return p;									// |dens| ‚â≤ 1e-6 in practice
}

Behaviour

    If the Newton step overshoots or hits a flat region, the back-tracking loop keeps halving the step until |dens| decreases.

    A ‚Äúflat spot‚Äù (gradient almost zero) nudges the sample 2 % of a unit distance toward the original point and tries again.

    Total dens() calls ‚â§ 1 + maxOuter¬∑(7 + backtracking); with maxOuter=8 the worst case is about 70 evaluations ‚Äî still tiny for GPU shaders.

How to choose
Function	dens() calls	Handles folds / flats?	Recommended use
nearestIsoNewton(p0,2)	15	No	Smooth, single-blob Gaussians
nearestIsoRobust(p0)	‚â§ 70	Yes	Sine-noise, ridges, overlapping bells

Drop either one next to your existing dens(vec3) and call it from your physics code or ray-marcher.

ChatGPT said:

Below is a single-call, four-sample gradient that matches your spec exactly.
Indentation uses tabs; no stray blanks inside for(...) {.

/* gradF4 :
   samples dens() at 4 vertices of a cube side-length 2h
   ( + + + ), ( + ‚Äì ‚Äì ), ( ‚Äì + ‚Äì ), ( ‚Äì ‚Äì + )
   ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ
   Returns ‚àáF(p) using only four dens() calls.
*/
vec3 gradF4(vec3 p){
	const float h      = exp2(-12.0);		// 2‚Åª¬π¬≤  ‚âà 0.00024414
	const float inv4h  = 1.0 / (4.0*h);		// 1 / 4h for all three axes
	
	// offset vectors
	const vec3 offsA = vec3( h, h, h);
	const vec3 offsB = vec3( h,-h,-h);
	const vec3 offsC = vec3(-h, h,-h);
	const vec3 offsD = vec3(-h,-h, h);
	
	// four field samples
	float fA = dens(p + offsA);
	float fB = dens(p + offsB);
	float fC = dens(p + offsC);
	float fD = dens(p + offsD);
	
	/* For each axis use: (f+ side) - (f‚àí side)
	   x+: A,B    x‚àí: C,D      ‚Üí  fA+fB - fC-fD
	   y+: A,C    y‚àí: B,D      ‚Üí  fA+fC - fB-fD
	   z+: A,D    z‚àí: B,C      ‚Üí  fA+fD - fB-fC   */
	return vec3(
		( (fA + fB) - (fC + fD) ) * inv4h,
		( (fA + fC) - (fB + fD) ) * inv4h,
		( (fA + fD) - (fB + fC) ) * inv4h
	);
}

    All four sample points are the same distance sqrt(3)*h from p, so the stencil is perfectly symmetric.

    Because every component re-uses the same four values, only four calls to dens() are needed instead of six.

// one-step Newton projection: uses dens(p) once + gradF4(p) once
vec3 nearestIsoNewtonStep(vec3 p0){
	float f = dens(p0);		// 1 dens() call
	vec3 g = gradF4(p0);	// 1 gradF4() call (4 dens() inside)
	float g2 = dot(g,g);	// avoid /0 if g2‚âà0 by caller check
	return p0 - g * (f / g2);
}

// one‚Äìstep Newton when we know |gradF| is safely > 0
vec3 nearestIsoNewtonStepFast(vec3 p0){
	vec3  g = gradF4(p0);				// gradient, 4 dens() calls
	float f = dens(p0);					// field value, 1 dens() call
	float invLen = inversesqrt(dot(g,g));	// 1/|g|
	/* displacement  =  f / |g|  ¬∑  g/|g|
	   =  f ¬∑ g ¬∑ (1/|g|)¬≤                        */
	return p0 - f * g * (invLen * invLen);
}

ChatGPT said:

//------- helpers ------------------------------------------------------------
vec4 dens4(vec4 p4){return vec4(p4.xyz,dens(p4.xyz));}	// idempotent

vec3 gradF4(vec3 p){									// 4-sample stencil
	const float h=exp2(-12.0);
	const float inv4h=1.0/(4.0*h);
	float fA=dens(p+vec3( h, h, h));
	float fB=dens(p+vec3( h,-h,-h));
	float fC=dens(p+vec3(-h, h,-h));
	float fD=dens(p+vec3(-h,-h, h));
	return vec3(
		(fA+fB-fC-fD)*inv4h,
		(fA+fC-fB-fD)*inv4h,
		(fA+fD-fB-fC)*inv4h
	);
}

vec3 nearestIsoNewtonStepUnit(vec3 p0){				// one Newton jump
	vec3 g=gradF4(p0);
	float gLen=length(g);
	vec3 normedDirection=-g/gLen;					// down the gradient
	float distanceMoveThatDirection=dens(p0)/gLen;
	return p0+normedDirection*distanceMoveThatDirection;
}
//-------------------------------------------------------------------------

/* isoSearchPopulation :
   builds up to 64 sampled points (vec4 xyz,f) around p0
   each new member comes from a one-step Newton jump or a midpoint-jump
   returns the xyz of the member with |dens| closest to 0,
   ties broken by geometric closeness to p0                                        */
vec3 isoSearchPopulation(vec3 p0){
	const int MAX_POP=64;
	vec4 popul[MAX_POP];
	int populCount=0;
	popul[populCount++]=dens4(vec4(p0,0.0));

	for(int i=0;i<MAX_POP && populCount<MAX_POP;i++){
		int a=i%populCount;									// cycle existing
		// 1) Newton step from an existing point
		vec3 nPoint=nearestIsoNewtonStepUnit(popul[a].xyz);
		popul[populCount++]=dens4(vec4(nPoint,0.0));
		if(populCount>=MAX_POP)break;

		// 2) midpoint of current & next (wrap around), then Newton
		int b=(a+1)%populCount;
		vec3 mid=0.5*(popul[a].xyz+popul[b].xyz);
		vec3 midJump=nearestIsoNewtonStepUnit(mid);
		popul[populCount++]=dens4(vec4(midJump,0.0));
	}

	// choose best: smallest |f|, then closest to p0
	int bestIdx=0;
	float bestAbs=abs(popul[0].w);
	float bestDist=length(popul[0].xyz-p0);
	for(int i=1;i<populCount;i++){
		float absF=abs(popul[i].w);
		float dist=length(popul[i].xyz-p0);
		bool better=(absF<bestAbs)||(absF==bestAbs&&dist<bestDist);
		if(better){bestIdx=i;bestAbs=absF;bestDist=dist;}
	}
	return popul[bestIdx].xyz;
}

ChatGPT said:
Big-picture idea ‚Äî turn ‚Äújust sample density‚Äù into ‚Äúwalk straight to the skin‚Äù

    Record every probe as a 4-number package.

        x, y, z store the place you sampled.

        w stores the value dens(x,y,z).
        A list of these little records is the population you keep adding to.

    How you turn one probe into a better probe (Newton jump).
    Look at the probe‚Äôs gradient (how steep the field climbs around it).

        Normalize that gradient so it becomes a pure ‚Äúoutward pointing‚Äù direction.

        Scale that unit vector by ‚Äúfield value √∑ gradient length‚Äù.
        That exact scalar is how far you must travel backwards along the gradient to hit the isosurface.

        Add the step to the old point ‚Üí you land practically on dens = 0.
        (Because we sample the gradient with a perfectly symmetric 4-point stencil, it only costs four extra dens() calls.)

    Grow a little cloud of candidates instead of relying on one shot.

        Start with the original query position (p0).

        For each record already in the population you may
        a. take its Newton jump and push that result into the list,
        b. pick it and one neighbour, take their midpoint, jump from there, push that too.

        Keep going until you have, say, 64 total records (an inexpensive cap).

    Pick the winner.

        Among all stored records find the one whose |dens| is smallest (that‚Äôs the one hugging the surface).

        If two are equally close to zero, choose the one geometrically nearest to the starting centre p0.

This is simply a search-by-projection strategy:

    The Newton jump is the fast, ‚Äúshoot straight down the normal‚Äù step that collapses the raw field value into real geometric distance.

    The midpoint trick sprinkles extra seeds so you don‚Äôt get stranded if the first guess happened to sit in a fold or on the wrong lobe of the surface.

    The population cap keeps the budget predictable: at most 64 √ó (1 field + 4 gradient) evaluations, well under the thousands you were spending before.

Once you understand that geometry, you can plug any higher-level heuristics on top (mutations, random jitter, SPSA directions, whatever) ‚Äî the key is that each new candidate costs only one density value plus one 4-sample gradient to land almost exactly on the isosurface.

ChatGPT said:
Can the GPU ‚Äúsee‚Äù for every ball each frame?

Yes‚Äîby a wide margin.
1 How many dens() calls would the per-ball ray-probe cost?
knob	value	comment
grid on the ball	32 √ó 16 = 512 rays	Heilmeier pattern is plenty for curvature < r/4
march steps per ray	30 average	early-out after zero-cross or max range
balls	100 (example)	scale linearly

calls / frame = 512 ¬∑ 30 ¬∑ 100 ‚âà 1.5 √ó 10‚Å∂
calls / second = 1.5 √ó 10‚Å∂ ¬∑ 60 ‚âà 9.2 √ó 10‚Å∑

You are already sustaining 1.8 √ó 10‚Åπ dens() evaluations per second in the main 640 √ó 480 √ó 100 ray-marcher. The per-ball sensor field would eat ‚âà 5 % of that budget, barely a blip.

Even a ten-fold jump to 1000 balls would push the cost to ‚âà 9 √ó 10‚Å∏ / s‚Äîstill only half of what your card is presently cruising through.
2 What you gain by knowing the nearest point q for every ball

Once you have the pair
(center p , closestSurfacePoint q)

    the signed distance is d = ||p-q|| (with inside/outside sign taken from dens(p)),

    the surface normal is simply
    n = normalize(p ‚àí q) (collinearity theorem we discussed),

    any local response depends on how dens() changes if you nudge the surface along n.

That last part is the expensive bit in your current finite-difference scheme: you perturb every one of the ~500 parameters and resample the whole loss.
But with q in hand you can fold the chain rule at the ball level:

‚àÇloss / ‚àÇparameter_k  =
        Œª  ¬∑  ‚àÇdens(q) / ‚àÇparameter_k
where   Œª  =  (r ‚àí d)+      // penetration depth or 0

    For a Gaussian hill, ‚àÇdens / ‚àÇcenter, ‚àÇdens / ‚àÇœÉ, ‚àÇdens / ‚àÇweight are closed-form and O(1).

    For the arbitrary ‚Äúuser shader‚Äù terms you can still use SPSA or dual-numbers, but now you need only one evaluation per term, not per ball √ó per term.

The work per frame becomes:

(ball-sensor)  +  Œ£ parameters (cheap local derivative)

versus your old

ball-sensor  √ó  (#parameters + 1)

That is roughly the same 20-fold win we saw analytically, now delivered by real ray data.
3 How to shoe-horn it into the existing pipeline

    Add a second draw call after the main render pass.
    Same vertex shader but emit rays from each ball center instead of pixels; write the found q into an off-screen RGBA texture (x,y,z,optional flag).

    Read back or bind that texture in the finite-difference / SPSA pass.
    You now have p, q, and n for every ball as uniforms or as a small texture.

    Compute per-parameter gradients on the CPU or in a tiny vertex loop that iterates over the 250 Gaussians + user extras, using the analytic formulas or a one-shot SPSA each.

Bottom line

    Throughput: Your card is already 20√ó faster than needed; the extra ray fan per ball is negligible.

    Physics cost: Knowing q collapses the gradient computation from O(#parameters √ó #balls) density calls to O(#parameters) lightweight arithmetic.

    Scalability: Because the probe grid and ray length are constants, doubling the ball count merely doubles a small additive term‚Äînot the whole frame time.

So, yes: let every ball ‚Äúlook‚Äù at its neighbourhood each frame; you have cycles to spare and you‚Äôll slash the expensive part of the physics step in the process.

You‚Äôre right ‚Äî you still need one nearest‚Äêsurface point q·µ¶ for every ball b, and each parameter‚Äôs gradient must accumulate contributions from all of those balls. What the ray-probe shortcut really changes is what counts as ‚Äúexpensive.‚Äù
Where the cost actually lands
Stage	Before (finite-difference on every parameter)	After (ray to find q·µ¶ first)
Find q·µ¶	‚Äî	one short ray-march per ball (tiny compared with your main 640√ó480√ó100 pass)
Derivative per (parameter k, ball b)	re-run the whole loss ‚áí one full dens() shot per pair	closed-form arithmetic for Gaussians, or one SPSA probe near q·µ¶ for ‚Äúwild‚Äù terms
Total heavy dens() calls per frame	O(P √ó B)	B √ó probeGrid (already dominated by your render)
Total FLOPs per frame	very high	still O(P √ó B), but each term is ~10 cheap FMAs instead of a 30-step ray-march

With 250 parameters √ó 100 balls:

    Old path: 25 000 full evaluations of the field (that‚Äôs what hurt).

    Ray-first path: 25 000 little dot-products / exponentials that cost about the same as a single dens() evaluation. The heavy work is now the 100 √ó 512 √ó 30 ‚âà 1.5 M steps you already budgeted as negligible.

So the asymptotic count, O(P √ó B), does stay the same, but the unit cost per pair collapses from ‚Äúanother field trace‚Äù to ‚Äúa handful of scalar multiplies.‚Äù That‚Äôs why the overall frame time drops even though the big-O label doesn‚Äôt.
In plain terms

    Each ball still needs its own surface hit so you know the normal that pushes back on it.

    Each parameter still feels every ball, because the loss is a sum over balls.

    But once you have the normals, the influence of a parameter on a given ball no longer requires marching a ray; it‚Äôs just plug-in-the-formula for a Gaussian (or one extra dens call right around that spot for a custom term).

    Those formulas are cheap enough that, even multiplied by 25 000, they fit easily inside the frame time you already reserve for graphics.

That‚Äôs the practical win: the expensive ray marches are decoupled from the parameter derivatives and paid only once per ball, not once per (ball, parameter) pair.

1 What ‚Äútotal-loss‚Äù usually means in this game

For each ball b
‚ÄÅ‚Ä¢ centre p_b ‚ÄÉ(3 floats)
‚ÄÅ‚Ä¢ nearest point on the surface q_b‚ÄÉ(found by ray-march or Newton)
‚ÄÅ‚Ä¢ signed gap d_b = ‚Äñp_b‚àíq_b‚Äñ‚ÄÉ(positive outside, negative inside)

Pick any penalty you like; a common one is a quadratic spring that activates only in penetration:

loss_b = ¬Ω ¬∑ max( radius ‚àí d_b , 0 )¬≤

The game‚Äôs overall loss is just the sum over balls

L = Œ£_b loss_b .

If a frame has 100 balls that are all clear of the skin, L is zero; when one or more poke in, the number grows exactly with the overlap depth.
2 Gradient of that loss

For every model parameter Œ∏‚Çñ (the 5 per bell, plus anything else) you need

‚àÇL/‚àÇŒ∏‚Çñ = Œ£_b  ( ‚àÇloss_b/‚àÇd_b ) ¬∑ ( ‚àÇd_b/‚àÇŒ∏‚Çñ )

    ‚àÇloss_b/‚àÇd_b is trivial:
    for the quadratic spring it is simply
    Œª_b = -(radius ‚àí d_b) if the ball is inside, and 0 if outside.

    ‚àÇd_b/‚àÇŒ∏‚Çñ is where the surface geometry comes in.
    Using the implicit-surface identity

dens(q_b) == 0  ‚ÄÉ‚áí  ‚ÄÉn_b¬∑u_Œ∏ + ‚àÇdens/‚àÇŒ∏‚Çñ (q_b) = 0

(u_Œ∏ is the way the surface point moves when you wiggle Œ∏‚Çñ)
you get

‚àÇd_b/‚àÇŒ∏‚Çñ = - ‚àÇdens/‚àÇŒ∏‚Çñ (q_b)  /  |‚àádens(q_b)| .

The denominator is just the length of the gradient you already have from the ray-march.

Putting the two pieces together:

‚àÇL/‚àÇŒ∏‚Çñ = Œ£_b  Œª_b ¬∑ [ - ‚àÇdens/‚àÇŒ∏‚Çñ (q_b) / |‚àádens(q_b)| ] .

3 Why this is much cheaper than perturb-every-parameter

    You still need one q_b per ball each frame.
    That costs a ray-march of maybe 30 steps along ~500 directions per ball.
    You‚Äôre already doing 1.8 billion dens() calls per second for rendering, so adding
    ‚Äú100 balls √ó 30 steps √ó 512 rays ‚âà 1.5 million calls‚Äù is < 5 % extra.

    But once every q_b is known, the big loop over parameters never ray-marches again.

        For a Gaussian primitive the derivative terms are closed-form:
        ‚àÇdens/‚àÇweight = exp(-r¬≤/2œÉ¬≤)
        ‚àÇdens/‚àÇcentre = weight ¬∑ (p-centre)/œÉ¬≤ ¬∑ exp()
        ‚àÇdens/‚àÇœÉ¬≤ = ‚Ä¶
        So each (ball,parameter) pair is a dozen FMAs and an exp, not another 30-step march.

        For ‚Äúwild‚Äù user-written bits you can still take one SPSA probe centred on q_b, but that‚Äôs one probe, independent of the number of balls.

In numbers:
250 parameters √ó 100 balls √ó 15 cheap-ops ‚âà 0.4 million FMAs ‚Äî a millisecond-class chore.
4 About ‚Äúshared ray-trace calls‚Äù

All balls can indeed be rendered in one low-res pass (say 320 √ó 160) by:

    Launching a vertex per ray, arranged in a 32 √ó 16 bloc per ball.

    Indexing into a UBO or texture that holds each ball‚Äôs centre.

    Writing the hit point (or ‚Äúno hit‚Äù) into an off-screen RGBA buffer.

That single draw call covers all balls and returns every q_b.
So you ‚Äúshare‚Äù the rasterisation overhead, but you still produce one shortest-distance march per ray. The savings come because you no longer need to re-launch that march for every parameter perturbation; you reuse the same hits when you compute the chain-rule sums.
5 Workflow in practice

    Ray-march once for all balls ‚Üí get q_b, n_b, |‚àádens|.

    CPU or a tiny vertex loop walks over the 250 parameters:

        evaluate closed-form derivatives at each q_b,

        multiply by Œª_b, accumulate into a 250-component gradient vector.

    Velocity update uses that vector just as before.

You have effectively traded ‚Äúlots of heavy field evaluations‚Äù for ‚Äúone extra render-style pass plus a heap of scalar math,‚Äù which your existing 60 FPS budget can easily absorb.

what if we do collisions between 2 isosurfaces (3d volume they contain) instead of between isosurface and ball. During raytracing we could sum amountCollision(densA(vec3),densB(vec3)) for some given func amountCollision(float,float)
ChatGPT said:

It can work, and the idea is sound.
Instead of asking ‚ÄúHow far is a sphere-centre from one surface?‚Äù you ask ‚ÄúHow much do the solid regions of two implicit objects overlap at this pixel / along this ray?‚Äù.
During the ray-march you evaluate the two scalar fields once per step, feed those two numbers into a tiny collision-kernel amountCollision(fA , fB), and accumulate the result.
The sum you end up with is zero when the two volumes are disjoint and grows smoothly as they interpenetrate.
1‚ÄÉChoosing amountCollision(fA , fB)
Goal	Simple formula that works
Penalise overlap only when both samples are inside their objects	max(‚àífA , 0) ¬∑ max(‚àífB , 0)
Want a softer fall-off (C¬π)	0.5 ¬∑ smoothstep(0, ‚àír, fA) ¬∑ smoothstep(0, ‚àír, fB)
Want a hard ‚Äúcontact‚Äù only when the two surfaces cross (C‚Å∞)	step(0,‚àífA) ¬∑ step(0,‚àífB)

Here ‚Äúinside‚Äù is defined as dens < 0; change the sign if your convention is opposite.
2‚ÄÉInterpreting the accumulated value

    If you march until min(fA , fB) > 0 (both outside) you can treat the integral as a penetration volume measured in voxels.

    If you march for a fixed distance you get an overlap score that is larger when the surfaces stay interleaved over a longer segment of the ray.

3‚ÄÉTurning that score into forces

Once per frame, for each object:

    Sum the scores of all rays whose first hit belongs to that object.

    The gradient of that sum with respect to the object‚Äôs parameters is

Œ£_rays   Œª_r   ¬∑  ‚àÇamountCollision(fA , fB) / ‚àÇparameter

where Œª_r is a sign that tells whether object A or B was the ‚Äúowner‚Äù of that ray‚Äêstep on the march stack.
If your objects are Gaussian sums, these partials are again analytic and cheap.
4‚ÄÉWhy it scales

    One scalar-field evaluation per object per step ‚Äì you were already paying that for visual rendering.

    No per-parameter ray marches ‚Äì the derivatives are closed-form (Gaussians) or at worst a single finite difference right at the sample point.

    Parallel over rays ‚Äì the same rasterised grid you use for shading can be reused; you just write into an overlap-buffer instead of the colour buffer.

5‚ÄÉCaveats

    You lose the simple ‚Äúsurface normal points to centre‚Äù geometry; the contact direction now comes from the gradient difference gradA ‚Äì gradB.

    If the two surfaces are very thin sheets that cross at oblique angles, a low-resolution ray grid can miss slivers. In practice a 32 √ó 16 fan per object face is plenty for unit-radius features at 60 FPS.

    For fractal or highly oscillatory extra terms you may still need a local SPSA probe at a few troublesome rays to keep the gradient stable.

Bottom line

    Yes ‚Äì sampling both fields once per ray-march step and folding them through a lightweight amountCollision() gives you a single pass overlap energy that replaces thousands of per-parameter marches.

    Stick to a smooth quadratic or smoothstep-based kernel so the gradient is continuous; you‚Äôll get better convergence in the integrator and the visual debug texture will be less speckled.

    Keep everything in the GPU ‚Üí frame-coherent.

    Favour deterministic approximations over exact-but-variable work.
    ‚ÄÉGames feel better when the step cost is stable; an occasional millimetre of penetration is OK, a sporadic 10 ms hitch is not.

Concrete plan I would ship with
Stage	What runs	Cost per frame (100 balls)	Why this choice
A. Ball ‚Üí surface sensing	One low-res ray fan per ball (e.g. 32√ó16, 30 steps)	‚âà 1.5 M dens() calls (‚ÄÜ<< render budget)	Gives each ball its closest hit point q, surface normal n, and gradient length.
B. Local gradients at q	On GPU in the same pass: analytic ‚àÇdens/‚àÇ(bell params) for Gaussians; 1-point SPSA for user ‚Äúwildcards.‚Äù	1 add-on texture fetch per parameter per ball ‚áí tiny	Removes need for 500 extra ray marches per parameter.

Why I prefer this to SPSA-only or full AD

    Ray fans double as a visual debug tool ‚Äì shade them and you literally ‚Äúsee‚Äù what each ball thinks the world looks like.

    Analytic Gaussians are near-free and cover most of the field‚Äôs complexity; only the user‚Äôs ad-hoc snippets fall back to SPSA.

    No huge tape or SSBOs ‚Äì fits the WebGL2 uniform + texture limits you outlined, and needs no browser features you don‚Äôt already use.

    Predictable frame cost ‚Äì each ball always shoots the same number of rays; no data-dependent iterations.

If I later need more balls

    Coarser ray grid per ball (say 16√ó8) ‚Äì halves cost while giving similar normals because curvature is bounded by ball radius.

    Batch balls in tiles ‚Äì one draw can shade 1024 small ray fans if the uniform blocks supply positions as a texture.

    Optional BVH culling on bells ‚Äì pays off only once you exceed ~500 bells.

Gameplay / editor perks

    Live surface sculpting: when the designer tweaks a bell weight, the very next frame‚Äôs physics sees it because the per-ball pass always fetches current bell parameters.

    Interactive contact heat-map: accumulate the per-ball overlap energy into a 2-D surface texture and overlay it ‚Äì designers instantly spot trouble spots.

    Cheap haptic feedback: the scalar penetration depth Œª you already compute can feed controller rumble without extra math.

    One extra low-res ray-probe pass per frame ‚á¢ q, n, |‚àáF| per ball.

so u can basically approximate signedDistance just by 4 dens(vec3) calls to get gradient vec3, when near the isosurface. what if the ball is in a hole shaped as another bigger ball, so in every direction theres isosurface, and its only a little off center in that
ChatGPT said:
Why the ‚Äú signedDistance ‚âà dens / |grad| ‚Äù trick fails at the bottom of a hole

The shortcut works only if two things are true at the sample point p :

    dens(p) is small (you are close to the surface).

    |grad dens|(p) is not near zero (the surface has a clear local slope there).

When a ball sits almost at the centre of a larger hollow sphere (or any highly symmetric cavity)

    dens(p) is indeed negative (you are inside), but

    the gradient vanishes ‚Äì every direction is equally ‚Äúuphill,‚Äù so
    |grad| ‚Üí 0 and the ratio dens / |grad| blows up or becomes numerically noisy.

You therefore need a fall-back that does more work whenever
|grad| < smallThreshold. Here are the usual options (no code, just the idea).
1‚ÄÉNewton with an initial nudge

    Detect flatness: if |grad(p)| < Œµ ¬∑ radius (Œµ‚âà10-3) switch strategy.

    Jitter p outward by a tiny random vector of length, say, two per-cent of the ball radius and recompute dens and grad.
    Inside a hollow sphere that small step already lands you on a slope where |grad| ‚â† 0, after which the normal Newton jump converges to the surface in 1‚Äì2 iterations.

    If the jitter overshoots the surface you still get a valid intersection‚Äîjust in the opposite direction‚Äîand the signed distance remains correct.

Cost:‚ÄÉone extra dens evaluation in the rare flat cases.
2‚ÄÉMini ‚Äúsphere-trace‚Äù when the gradient is tiny

A safer but slightly dearer fall-back:

    Pick the direction of the largest absolute component of grad (if all are near zero, choose any axis; you are almost at the centre).

    March a short ray along that axis in fixed steps h until dens changes sign.

    Linear-interpolate between the last two samples to locate the zero crossing.

In a unit-radius cavity ten steps of size 0.1 already hit the surface within 0.01 units.
Because balls rarely sit perfectly in the dead centre, this slow path triggers only occasionally.

Cost:‚ÄÉ‚â§10 extra dens calls in worst-case frames.
3‚ÄÉAnalytic shortcut for Gaussians (when you know the primitive)

For a single 3-D Gaussian hill or hole:

dens(p) = w ¬∑ exp( -|p ‚àí c|¬≤ / (2 œÉ¬≤) )

If the ball is inside (w negative for a hole) and gradient is zero at p = c, the distance to the dens = 0 surface is simply

distance = œÉ ¬∑ sqrt( 2 ¬∑ log( |w| / threshold ) )

where threshold is the iso-value you use for the surface (often 0).
In practice games blend several Gaussians, so this analytic radius is more of a quick estimate to choose between ‚Äúneed robust method‚Äù or not.
4‚ÄÉHybrid rule-of-thumb I would implement

    Compute dens and the 4-sample gradient at the ball centre.

    If |dens| < 0.25 ¬∑ radius and |grad| > 1e-3 ‚Üí use the fast ratio dens / |grad|.

    Else if |grad| > 1e-4 ‚Üí do a single Newton jump; accept its result.

    Else ‚Üí run the 10-step axial sphere-trace fall-back.
    (This fires only for near-perfect symmetry or when a ball starts exactly at the centre of a bowl.)

The whole decision tree costs at most a dozen extra dens() evaluations in pathological frames, which is negligible next to the millions you budget for rendering.
What you gain

    Stable numbers ‚Äì no ‚Äúdivide by almost zero‚Äù spikes in the physics, so no jitter in ball motion.

    Deterministic runtime ‚Äì you always know the worst-case count of additional dens() calls.

    No changes to the main render shader ‚Äì the fall-back lives entirely in the physics pass.

So, while the four-sample gradient trick is great 99 % of the time, always pair it with a flat-spot escape hatch for cavities or symmetry centres; the cost is tiny, and correctness is guaranteed.
